### 488. Preserve Old Endpoints For Backward Compatibility
**Rule:** When modifying or replacing an API endpoint, keep the old endpoint functional for backward compatibility, especially when external consumers or agents may depend on it.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #40505
**Modules:** executor
**Trigger:** You're modifying or replacing an API endpoint in the executor or any service where external consumers, agents, or other microservices depend on the existing contract.
**Rationale:** Removing or replacing an endpoint without preserving the old one breaks external consumers and agents that depend on the existing contract, causing runtime failures that are difficult to diagnose and coordinate fixes for across teams.

---

### 489. Preserve Old Impl During Migration
**Rule:** When migrating between SDK versions behind a feature flag, keep the old implementation unchanged so it remains a valid fallback; do not modify the old path with new SDK calls.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #41063
**Modules:** server
**Trigger:** You're migrating between SDK versions (e.g., v1 to v2) behind a feature flag, and both the old and new code paths must coexist until the migration is fully rolled out.
**Rationale:** Modifying the old code path with new SDK calls couples your fallback to the new dependency, so if the new SDK has bugs or breaking changes, the feature flag no longer provides a safe rollback. Keeping the old implementation pristine ensures the flag is a true circuit breaker, not just a cosmetic branch over shared broken code.

---

### 490. Preserve Test Coverage During Refactors
**Rule:** When moving or refactoring code, verify that all existing test assertions are preserved in the new location — don't silently drop test conditions.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3

**Modules:** domino-cost, model-serving

### 491. Promote Reusable Components To Shared

### 497. Remove Inapplicable Fields From Entities

### 499. Simplify By Requiring Parameters

### 500. Reserve Usex Prefix For Hooks Only

### 501. Restrict Interface Exposure To Control Injection Scope

### 502. Return Null Instead Of Empty Dom Elements

### 503. Return Types Must Reflect Actual Side Effects
**Rule:** If a method mutates a builder or performs side effects and callers discard the return value, the return type should be Unit — a non-Unit return type implies a pure transformation the caller should use.

### 506. Separate Business Logic From Presentation

### 508. Separate Pagination Sorting Filtering

---

### 509. Separate Validation Helpers From Domain Entities
**Rule:** Validation helpers and required-field definitions are not domain entities; extract them into a dedicated file (e.g., ValidationHelpers) scoped to the package that uses them, keeping domain entity files focused on business concepts.

**Category:** code-organization
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #40794
**Modules:** projects

---

### 510. Service Encapsulates Full Lifecycle
**Rule:** A service method for a create or update operation should encapsulate its full lifecycle (database persistence and external resource management) rather than splitting responsibilities across the controller and service layers.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #40765
**Modules:** model-serving

---

### 511. Services Return Optionals
**Rule:** Services should return Optional values and let the caller decide how to handle absence, rather than throwing exceptions internally — the correct failure mode (IllegalArgument vs IllegalState) depends on the caller's context.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #40784
**Modules:** projects

### 512. Set Default Values At Parameter Destructuring
**Rule:** Prefer setting default values inline at the destructuring site rather than using separate conditional logic or fallback assignments later in the function body.

### 513. Set Safe Limits For Non Paginated Endpoints
**Rule:** For non-paginated endpoints, set the internal query limit high enough that it is effectively unreachable, to prevent items from being silently dropped from the response.

**Category:** api-design

**Confidence:** 0.3

### 514. Simplify Redundant Boolean Expressions
**Rule:** Review compound boolean conditions for redundancy; e.g., `(loading && !hasLoaded) || !hasLoaded` reduces to just `!hasLoaded`.

**Category:** code-organization

### 515. State Updates Only On Success Path
**Rule:** Side effects like changing user selection or UI state after an async operation must be gated on the success path, not executed unconditionally after the operation completes.

### 516. Stable Keys Not Array Index

### 517. State Variable Names Must Reflect Contents
**Rule:** State variables should be named to accurately reflect all the data they contain; a state object holding pagination, sorting, and filter state should not be named just 'pagination' as it misleads readers about its scope.

**Category:** naming
**Mode:** ambient

---

### 518. Use Ant Form For State Management Not Just Styling
**Rule:** When using Ant Design Form components, let the Form manage field state rather than using it purely as a layout/styling wrapper with separate state management.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #41523, #42763
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're building a form using Ant Design's Form components and you're tempted to manage field values with separate useState hooks or a custom state object while only using Form.Item for layout and validation styling.
**Rationale:** Ant Design's Form component already provides a complete state management layer — field tracking, validation, dirty detection, and submission gating. Duplicating that with useState creates two sources of truth, increases bug surface area from sync drift, and throws away built-in features like cross-field validation, scroll-to-error, and form-level reset.

### 519. Todo Comments For Temporary Code

**Category:** documentation

### 520. Test Positive And Negative Conditional Config
**Rule:** When configuration (e.g., env vars) is conditionally included, write both a positive test (present when condition is met) and a negative test (absent when condition is not met).

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #45586, #41466, #46554
**Modules:** server, frontend, gen-ai
**Trigger:** You're writing or reviewing tests for code that conditionally includes configuration—such as environment variables, feature flags, or optional settings—based on some condition (e.g., a boolean flag, a deployment mode, or a user setting).
**Rationale:** Without a negative test, broken conditional logic can silently leak configuration into contexts where it shouldn't exist—causing security exposure (leaked credentials), misconfiguration (wrong proxy routes), or subtle runtime errors that only surface in production.

---

### 532. Use Collect With Pattern Matching For Partial Transformations
**Rule:** When filtering and transforming a collection simultaneously, use `.collect` with pattern matching instead of chaining `.filter` and `.map` separately.

---

### 533. Use Bulk Database Queries Instead Of Iterative Individual Lookups
**Rule:** When checking existence or fetching multiple records, use a single bulk query with $in or similar operators and do in-memory diffing, rather than issuing one query per item.

**Category:** performance
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #40900, #47156
**Modules:** projects, extensions
**Trigger:** You're writing code that checks existence or fetches data for multiple items, and you find yourself calling the database inside a loop or mapping over a collection with individual queries.
**Rationale:** Issuing one query per item creates N+1 performance problems that scale linearly with collection size, causing unnecessary database round-trips, connection pool pressure, and latency spikes under load. A single bulk query with in-memory diffing reduces this to constant database overhead regardless of collection size.

---

### 534. Use Canonical Import Paths Not Dist Paths
**Rule:** Import from the package's canonical alias (e.g., ui/) rather than reaching into the dist build output (e.g., @domino/ui/dist/).

### 541. Use Enums For Multi State Flags Instead Of Booleans
**Rule:** When a value has more than two meaningful states (e.g., waiting, requested, completed), use an enum or sealed type rather than a boolean, and name it to capture the intent of each transition.

**Category:** naming
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #40505
**Modules:** executor
**Trigger:** You're adding a flag or status field that could take on more than two meaningful states, or you notice an existing boolean whose name hints at a richer lifecycle (e.g., `isRequested`, `isCompleted`).
**Rationale:** Booleans create an implicit combinatorial state space (2^n) where most combinations are invalid. An enum makes each legitimate state explicit, lets the compiler enforce exhaustive handling, and keeps the code readable as the lifecycle grows.

### 548. Use Shared Mock Utilities For Component Mocking

**Rationale:** Inline mocks that render static markup discard all prop and callback information, making it impossible to verify that parent components wire up handlers correctly. Shared mock utilities preserve prop access, eliminate duplicated boilerplate across test files, and make mock behavior consistent across the test suite.

### 556. Useeffect Stable Dependency Inclusion

### 557. Validate All Inputs Before Mutating
**Rule:** Validate all inputs before making any state changes so that the operation is atomic — either all changes succeed or none are applied.

**Category:** architecture

**Modules:** projects, apps

---

### 558. Validate Build Artifact Assumptions In Package Links
**Rule:** When linking monorepo packages, verify that assumed build output directories (e.g., /dist) will exist at resolution time and document the dependency on the build step.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #39589
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're adding or modifying a `link:` or `file:` dependency in a monorepo package.json that points to another local package, or you're updating the build output configuration of a package that other packages link to.
**Rationale:** Link and file references in package.json resolve at install time to the target directory as-is, without triggering a build. If the assumed output directory doesn't exist yet—common after a fresh clone or CI cache miss—imports silently fail with confusing module-not-found errors that waste significant debugging time.

---

### 559. Verify Aria Attributes When Spreading Props To Multiple Elements
**Rule:** When using rest/spread props that include accessibility attributes like aria-label, verify the attribute is interpreted correctly on each target element, especially when both a link and a button receive the same spread props.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #40861
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're spreading rest props (e.g., `...rest` or `...props`) onto multiple elements within a component, and those props may include accessibility attributes like `aria-label`, `aria-describedby`, or `role`.
**Rationale:** When spread props containing accessibility attributes are applied to multiple elements, each element inherits attributes intended for a different context, causing screen readers to announce incorrect or confusing labels. Destructuring accessibility props and applying them to the correct target element ensures each interactive element has an accurate accessible name.

---

### 560. Verify Backwards Compatibility When Removing Fields
**Rule:** When removing a field from an API or service interface, verify that all known clients do not depend on it before merging.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #46586, #47262
**Modules:** jobs, apps
**Trigger:** You're removing or deprecating a field from an API response, request payload, or service interface. You're also refactoring a shared model where downstream consumers may rely on fields you plan to delete.
**Rationale:** Removing a field from a shared interface without verifying all consumers causes silent runtime failures that bypass compile-time and test-time checks. In a multi-service architecture like Domino's, the blast radius of a removed field extends across service boundaries to UI clients, SDKs, and integrations that may not be covered by the same CI pipeline.

---

### 561. Verify Role Based Permissions Match Expected Access Levels
**Rule:** When modifying permission or role definitions, explicitly verify that each role (especially restricted roles like limitedAdmin) has the expected access level and cannot view or modify resources beyond its intended scope.

**Category:** security
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #42394, #42264
**Modules:** server, compute-providers
**Trigger:** You're modifying permission definitions, role hierarchies, or access control checks—especially when adding new roles, changing existing role capabilities, or refactoring authorization logic.
**Rationale:** Permission bugs are silent and high-impact—a role that can see or modify resources beyond its scope is a security vulnerability that won't surface until an audit or breach. Explicit negative test cases for each restricted role catch privilege escalation before it reaches production.

---

### 562. Verify Tests When Modifying Shared Code Paths
**Rule:** When modifying a handler or function used by multiple callers, verify that existing integration/e2e tests covering all call sites still pass before merging.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.3
**Review count:** 3
**Source PRs:** #41094, #43868
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 563. Add Negative Authorization Test Cases
**Rule:** When testing authorization changes, include negative test cases that verify users cannot perform actions outside their allowed scope (e.g., transferring to an org they don't belong to).

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #43219, #41140
**Modules:** environments, dataset-rw
**Trigger:** You're writing or modifying tests for authorization logic, such as role-based access control, org membership checks, or permission gates on API endpoints.
**Rationale:** Authorization bugs are among the most critical security vulnerabilities — a missing negative test means a broken permission check can ship undetected. Negative tests catch regressions where a refactor accidentally removes or weakens a scope check, preventing unauthorized data access or privilege escalation.

---

### 564. Abstract Provider Specific Details From User Facing Apis
**Rule:** When designing APIs that support multiple providers or backends, accept generalized parameter names from the user and map them internally to provider-specific fields, reducing user-facing complexity.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42382
**Modules:** aigateway
**Trigger:** You're designing or modifying an API endpoint in the AI Gateway that accepts configuration for multiple LLM providers or backends. You need to decide whether to expose provider-specific parameter names directly to the caller or use a generalized interface.
**Rationale:** Leaking provider-specific parameter names into the public API couples every caller to each provider's naming conventions, making it error-prone to switch providers and forcing breaking API changes whenever a new backend is added. A generalized interface keeps the contract stable and shifts mapping complexity to an internal layer that is easier to test and extend.

---

### 565. Accurate Component Prop Types
**Rule:** When wrapping a component, only declare prop types you actually forward; use Pick to select forwarded props, or use the rest operator to pass through remaining props.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41587
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're creating a wrapper component around an existing component and need to define its prop types. You're tempted to extend or duplicate the full prop interface of the inner component even though your wrapper only forwards a subset of those props.
**Rationale:** When a wrapper's type interface advertises props it never forwards, consumers write code that compiles cleanly but has no effect at runtime. Accurate prop types turn silent bugs into compile-time errors and make the wrapper's actual API surface immediately obvious to other developers.

---

### 566. Accurate Telemetry Event Names

**Category:** naming

### 573. Add Tests When Introducing New Interface Methods

### 579. Api Layer Should Not Format Errors
**Rule:** Pure API call functions should throw errors and let the caller handle error messaging; API layers should have no concern about user-facing error messages.

**Category:** error-handling

**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41893
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 580. Archive Dont Delete Referenced Entities
**Rule:** When removing entities that are referenced by other records (e.g., resource configurations referenced by model deployments), archive them instead of deleting to preserve referential integrity.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41346
**Modules:** compute-providers
**Trigger:** You're writing code to remove or clean up entities (e.g., resource configurations, hardware tiers, compute clusters) that may be referenced by other records such as model deployments, workspaces, or scheduled jobs.
**Rationale:** Hard-deleting entities that are referenced by other records creates dangling references, leading to runtime failures in deployments, workspaces, or jobs that depend on the deleted record. Archiving preserves data integrity while still removing the entity from active use.

### 581. Assert Result In Valid Set

### 593. Avoid Let And Value Overwriting
**Rule:** Avoid using `let` with subsequent value overwriting; instead, extract the logic into a function that returns the final value to promote immutability and clarity.

### 594. Capture Feature Flag Value At Resource Creation Time
**Rule:** When a feature flag controls infrastructure behavior (e.g., creating an ingress), capture the flag value into the resource record at creation time and use that persisted value for all subsequent operations, rather than re-reading the current flag value.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #46146
**Modules:** apps
**Trigger:** You're writing code where a feature flag controls how an infrastructure resource (e.g., ingress, service, volume) is created, and that resource will later need to be updated or deleted using the same configuration.
**Rationale:** When a feature flag is toggled after resource creation, re-reading the current flag value causes lifecycle operations (update, delete, reconcile) to act on a different configuration than what was actually provisioned — leading to orphaned infrastructure, failed deletions, or silent mismatches between the database and the real world.

---

### 616. Batch High Frequency Database Writes
**Rule:** When consuming a stream of events that each triggers a database write, consider batching writes (e.g., update after N events or within a time window) to reduce write churn.

**Category:** performance
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41303
**Modules:** server
**Trigger:** You're consuming a stream of events (e.g., status updates, metric reports, progress ticks) where each event triggers an individual database write or update.
**Rationale:** High-frequency individual writes cause excessive database round-trips, connection pool exhaustion, and row-level lock contention. Batching amortizes the overhead across many events, reducing total write operations by an order of magnitude while keeping data freshness within an acceptable window.

---

### 617. Batch Related State Updates Into A Single Call
**Rule:** When setting multiple fields on the same form or state object, combine them into a single call rather than making multiple sequential calls.

**Category:** performance
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41164
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 618. Boolean Method Names Should Be Precise
**Rule:** Boolean-returning methods should use names that clearly convey both the condition being checked and its specificity (e.g., `areMultipleStoragesRegistered` instead of ambiguous alternatives).

**Category:** naming
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41695
**Modules:** dataset-rw

---

### 619. Branch On Specific Signals Not Broad State
**Rule:** When handling process termination or state transitions, branch on the specific signal that caused the transition (e.g., stop properties being set) rather than the current broad state, to avoid catching unrelated exit reasons.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41576
**Modules:** executor
**Trigger:** You're writing termination or state-transition logic that needs to distinguish why a process ended or changed state. You're tempted to check the current broad state (e.g., "is it stopped?") instead of inspecting the specific signal that caused the transition.
**Rationale:** Branching on broad state conflates unrelated exit reasons under a single code path, causing silent mishandling when new termination signals are added or when different signals require fundamentally different recovery strategies. Matching on the specific causal signal makes each handler's scope explicit and forces compile-time or review-time attention when new signals appear.

---

### 620. Call Hooks At Top Level Not Inside Data Structures
**Rule:** Call React hooks at the top level of a component and assign results to variables before using them in arrays, objects, or other data structures — never invoke hooks inline within data structure definitions.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41878
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 621. Centralize Color Definitions
**Rule:** Color values should be defined in the centralized colors/theme list, not hardcoded or hidden in individual component files.

**Category:** code-organization
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #45523, #44601
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 622. Centralize Duplicated Sanitization Logic On Domain Objects
**Rule:** When the same transformation or sanitization logic appears in multiple places, move it onto the relevant domain object so consumers get the behavior for free.

**Category:** code-organization
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40783
**Modules:** projects
**Trigger:** You're writing or reviewing code where the same transformation or sanitization logic (e.g., trimming whitespace, normalizing casing, stripping invalid characters) is applied to a field in multiple call sites—controllers, services, or API handlers.
**Rationale:** Duplicated sanitization logic is a consistency bug waiting to happen—when one call site is updated but others are missed, the system silently accepts unsanitized data. Centralizing it on the domain object enforces the invariant at construction time, making it impossible for consumers to forget.

---

### 623. Centralize Encoding Utilities

### 630. Common Components Must Not Import Feature Specific Code
**Rule:** Global/shared components must never depend on feature-scoped components; if a feature-specific component is needed by a shared component, extract it into the common layer first.

### 631. Consider Database Migration For Field Renames
**Rule:** When renaming a field in BSON or database storage, consider writing a migration rather than just changing the field name in code, to avoid breaking reads of existing documents.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #46381, #41380
**Modules:** server
**Trigger:** You're renaming a field in a case class or model that maps to a BSON/MongoDB document. You need to ensure existing documents with the old field name are still readable after deployment.
**Rationale:** MongoDB documents written before a field rename still contain the old field name. Without a migration, reads of existing documents will fail with missing-field errors or silently return defaults, causing runtime failures that only surface in production with real data.

---

### 632. Coordinate Cross Repo Changes For Shared Constants
**Rule:** When changing ports, constants, or configuration in one repo, identify and create matching PRs in dependent repos (e.g., workload operator, compute services) before merging.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #43875, #42019
**Modules:** server, .circleci
**Trigger:** You're modifying a port number, shared constant, environment variable, or configuration value in one repository that other services depend on — for example, changing a default port in the server repo that the workload operator or compute services also reference.
**Rationale:** Shared constants like ports and config values create implicit contracts between repos. Changing one side without updating dependents causes silent deployment failures, broken inter-service communication, and CI environments that no longer reflect production — issues that are expensive to diagnose because the root cause spans multiple repositories.

---

### 644. Composition Over Modification
**Rule:** When a component needs additional data for a new use case, create a specialized wrapper that composes the original component rather than modifying it to fetch the data internally.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #43610, #40971
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're adding a new use case to an existing component that requires additional data or props not needed by the original. You're tempted to add a fetch call or new data dependency directly inside the existing component.
**Rationale:** Modifying an existing component to fetch new data couples all consumers to that data dependency, increasing bundle size, unnecessary network requests, and risk of regressions. Composition keeps the original component pure and testable while letting each use case supply exactly the data it needs.

---

### 645. Config Keys Match Code References
**Rule:** Configuration key names must exactly match the names referenced in the code that reads them; mismatches cause silent failures.

**Category:** naming
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41187
**Modules:** server

---

### 646. Consider All Api Consumers
**Rule:** When modifying a shared API endpoint, identify all consumers (UI, other services) and ensure they are updated to match the new contract.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #46660, #47263
**Modules:** apps, nucleus
**Trigger:** You're modifying a shared API endpoint's request or response shape in nucleus or apps, and the endpoint is consumed by the UI, CLI, or other internal services.
**Rationale:** Shared API endpoints form implicit contracts with every consumer. Changing the contract without updating all callers causes silent runtime failures — blank UI fields, broken CLI commands, or service errors — that often aren't caught until production because each consumer's tests only cover its own layer.

---

### 647. Consider Audit Event Volume For High Frequency Operations
**Rule:** Before adding audit events to an operation, evaluate how frequently it fires in practice — high-frequency triggers (e.g., file preview clicks) can create excessive noise.

**Category:** logging
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41343, #47177
**Modules:** audit_trail, server

---

### 648. Consider Credential Lifecycle On Soft Delete
**Rule:** When soft-deleting entities that have associated secrets or credentials, explicitly decide whether those credentials should be purged or retained, since users may expect their stored credentials are fully removed when they delete a resource.

**Category:** security
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41985
**Modules:** compute-providers
**Trigger:** You're implementing or modifying a soft-delete operation on an entity that has associated secrets, credentials, or API keys stored alongside it (e.g., compute provider configurations, data source connections, or service accounts).
**Rationale:** When users delete a resource, they reasonably expect associated credentials are fully removed — orphaned secrets create a hidden attack surface and may violate compliance requirements around credential retention. Failing to address credential lifecycle on soft-delete leads to stale secrets that accumulate over time and can be exploited if the secrets store is ever compromised.

---

### 649. Consistent Api Endpoint Descriptions
**Rule:** Use consistent phrasing across similar API endpoint descriptions (e.g., if one list endpoint says 'based on the provided filters', all similar list endpoints should use the same wording).

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2

**Modules:** compute-providers

### 650. Consistent Api Naming Across Services
**Rule:** When building new API endpoints or schemas for a feature that parallels an existing feature, use the same field names and conventions to maintain cross-API consistency, even if a more accurate name exists.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2

### 651. Consistent Audit Event Naming Convention
**Rule:** When designing audit/domain events, choose a consistent convention (unified event with a changes field vs. separate events per action) and apply it across the codebase to ensure queryable and coherent audit trails.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #43522, #43132
**Modules:** organizations, server
**Trigger:** You're adding or modifying audit events or domain events for a feature, especially when multiple actions (create, update, delete) need to be tracked in an audit trail.
**Rationale:** Inconsistent audit event conventions force consumers to handle multiple event shapes for the same domain, making audit trails harder to query, aggregate, and display. A uniform convention ensures that audit log UIs, compliance reports, and analytics pipelines can process events generically without per-event-type special-casing.

### 741. Fix Root Cause Instead Of Adding Long Test Timeouts
**Rule:** When tests are slow or flaky, investigate and fix the underlying cause (e.g., unmocked API calls, missing mocks) rather than increasing timeouts.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #43827, #44298
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're dealing with a slow or flaky test and considering increasing the timeout to make it pass. You notice a test that already has an unusually high timeout value.
**Rationale:** Long timeouts mask real problems like unmocked network calls, making tests slow in CI and prone to intermittent failures. Fixing the root cause produces fast, deterministic tests that catch actual regressions instead of racing against arbitrary time limits.

### 754. Guard Against Assumed Required Fields
**Rule:** When accessing a field assumed to always be present (e.g., via .get on an Option), add defensive handling or document the invariant, since 'always present' assumptions can silently break at runtime.

**Category:** error-handling

**Source PRs:** #47265
**Modules:** apps

---

### 755. Guard Against Request Queuing On Inter Service Calls
**Rule:** When adding periodic or synchronous calls to external services, consider whether requests can queue up and block other operations; apply timeouts to bound the impact.

**Category:** performance
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41485
**Modules:** projects
**Trigger:** You're adding or modifying code that makes periodic, scheduled, or synchronous HTTP calls to another internal service (e.g., a polling loop, health check, or status sync). You need to ensure that slow or unresponsive downstream services don't cause requests to pile up and starve the calling service's thread pool.
**Rationale:** When outbound service calls lack timeouts and concurrency guards, a single slow or unresponsive downstream service can cause requests to queue up, exhaust the calling service's thread pool, and cascade into failures for all other operations — effectively turning a downstream latency spike into a full service outage.

---

### 756. Loading State Flash Awareness
**Rule:** Be aware that checking loading=false and data=undefined together can cause a brief flash of incorrect UI state before data arrives; guard against this race condition in data-fetching patterns.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40720, #42761
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 757. Keep Annotations Accurate

### 758. Keep Cross Repo Config In Sync
**Rule:** When updating shared configuration values like image tags, ensure the same value is updated in all repositories and charts that reference it to maintain congruence.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40446, #42935
**Modules:** server, compute-providers
**Trigger:** You're updating a shared configuration value such as a Docker image tag, service version, or feature flag default that is referenced in multiple repositories or Helm charts. You need to ensure every location that consumes this value reflects the same change.
**Rationale:** Configuration drift between repositories causes silent runtime failures — services may reference incompatible versions of each other, leading to hard-to-diagnose issues in staging or production. Keeping all references in sync at PR time prevents deployment mismatches and reduces incident response burden.

### 763. Handle Partial Failure In Multi Call Sequences
**Rule:** When executing multiple sequential service calls that must all succeed for a consistent state, handle partial failures gracefully and ensure the UI reflects the actual state (e.g., refetch data after errors).

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #45796, #40750
**Modules:** frontend, projects
**Trigger:** You're writing a function or handler that makes multiple sequential API/service calls where all must succeed for the UI to reflect a consistent state, such as creating a resource and then updating its configuration.
**Rationale:** When a multi-step operation partially succeeds, the UI can silently diverge from server state, leaving users confused about what actually happened and unable to recover. Refetching after errors and reporting the specific failure point keeps the UI honest and gives users a clear path forward.

### 781. Isolate Changes With Null Object Pattern
**Rule:** When adding cross-cutting policies or behaviors, use a no-op/null implementation for modules that should not be affected to prevent unintended side effects.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42187
**Modules:** repocloner
**Trigger:** You're adding a cross-cutting concern (e.g., rate limiting, auditing, access control) that should apply to some modules but not others, and you need a clean way to exempt certain modules without scattering conditional logic.
**Rationale:** The Null Object pattern eliminates boolean flags and conditional branches for cross-cutting concerns, making exemptions explicit at the wiring layer rather than buried in module logic. This prevents accidental side effects when a policy is added globally but certain modules like repocloner should remain unaffected.

### 792. Keep File Names Version Agnostic

**Category:** naming

### 793. Keep Generated Api Specs In Sync

### 794. Keep Mapping Logic In Owning Service

### 800. Lifecycle Methods Should Come In Symmetric Pairs

### 801. Lockfile Changes Must Match Package.Json

### 831. No Redundant Telemetry Fields

**Modules:** server, guardrails

---

### 832. Polymorphism Over Optional Fields
**Rule:** When different entity subtypes have distinct sets of fields, use polymorphism (separate subclasses) instead of making all fields optional on a single type; this enforces correctness at compile time.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40794, #47124
**Modules:** projects, extensions
**Trigger:** You're defining or modifying a model/case class where different variants of an entity have distinct sets of fields, and you're tempted to make some fields Optional to accommodate all variants in a single type.
**Rationale:** Optional fields for subtype-specific data push variant enforcement to runtime, where forgotten checks cause NoneExceptions or silent bugs. Polymorphic subtypes make illegal states unrepresentable, letting the compiler catch missing cases at build time rather than in production.

---

### 833. No Unsafe Option Get
**Rule:** Never call `.get` on an `Option` type; use pattern matching (`match { case None => ... case Some(v) => ... }`) or a safe alternative like `.getOrElse`/`.fold` to handle the empty case explicitly.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #45031, #40900
**Modules:** apps, projects

---

### 834. Prefer Flexible Dependency Version Specifiers
**Rule:** Use >= rather than ~= for Python dependency pinning in requirements files to avoid breakage when transitive dependencies shift, unless a known incompatibility requires a tighter bound.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41395, #41415
**Modules:** skeletons, system-tests

---

### 861. Prefer Case Classes Over Annotated Classes
**Rule:** In Scala, prefer case classes over regular classes with @unused annotations; case class constructor parameters are automatically public vals, eliminating the need for boilerplate getters and suppression annotations.

### 870. Prefer Functional Combinators Over Manual Pattern Matching
**Rule:** Use map, flatMap, and other functional combinators on Either/Option/Try types instead of manually matching on left/right or some/none when the transformation is straightforward.

### 873. Prefer If Over And Chaining
**Rule:** Prefer if/ternary expressions over chaining && expressions when it avoids the need for non-null assertions (!).

**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2

**Modules:** frontend

---

### 874. Prefer Narrow Roles Over Adding Permissions To Broad Roles
**Rule:** When adding new feature permissions, create a dedicated role rather than adding them to an existing broad role, to avoid granting permissions to users who do not need them.

**Category:** security
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42758, #44372
**Modules:** server, remote-file-system
**Trigger:** You're adding a new feature that requires access control and you're considering adding a new permission to an existing broad role like 'ProjectAdmin' or 'DominoOperator'. You need to decide where to place the new permission grant.
**Rationale:** Adding permissions to broad roles violates the principle of least privilege — every user with that role gains access to features they may not need, increasing the blast radius of compromised accounts and making it harder to audit who can do what. Narrow, feature-scoped roles make permission grants explicit and revocable without side effects.

---

### 875. Prefer Immutable Array Methods
**Rule:** Prefer immutable array methods like `slice` over mutating methods like `splice`, even when working with copies, to signal immutable intent and prevent accidental mutation bugs.

**Category:** performance
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41992
**Modules:** frontend

### 897. Redact Pii In Telemetry Events

**Rationale:** Telemetry events are forwarded to third-party analytics platforms outside Domino's security boundary. Leaking PII such as usernames and emails to these services violates data privacy requirements and can create compliance exposure under GDPR, SOC 2, and customer data agreements.

### 921. Review Access Control On Data Endpoints
**Rule:** When an endpoint returns data filtered by user permissions, explicitly verify that unauthenticated or non-user callers cannot access restricted data, and raise security concerns in review if unclear.

**Category:** security
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41762
**Modules:** dataset-rw
**Trigger:** You're writing or reviewing an endpoint that returns data filtered by the caller's permissions, such as listing datasets, files, or resources scoped to a user or project.
**Rationale:** When permission filtering is the only access gate, unauthenticated or impersonated callers can receive an unfiltered superset of data. Explicit authentication enforcement ensures that missing credentials produce a hard rejection, not a silent data leak.

### 923. Safe Default For Staged Rollout
**Rule:** When introducing new configuration values that affect routing or paths, default to the safe/backward-compatible value first and change it in a follow-up PR to prevent breaking production.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40708
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're introducing a new configuration value, feature flag, or environment variable that affects routing, file paths, or API endpoints in a frontend application. The new value will change behavior from the current production baseline.
**Rationale:** Defaulting to the new value means every environment that hasn't explicitly set the config — including production — silently adopts the new behavior on deploy. Defaulting to the safe/legacy value gives you a two-step rollout with a clean rollback point between PRs, preventing surprise breakage in production.

### 943. Start Simple Extend Later
**Rule:** When a simpler API shape (e.g., single-value parameter) satisfies current requirements and expanding it later (e.g., to multi-value) is not a breaking change, ship the simpler version first.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40900
**Modules:** projects

---

### 944. Store Settimeout Id For Cleanup
**Rule:** Always capture the return value of `setTimeout` (the timeout ID) and use it with `clearTimeout` for proper cleanup; cancel the timeout when the async operation completes.

**Category:** error-handling

### 948. Test All Fields And No Fields For Optional Params
**Rule:** When making API fields optional, write at least two tests: one that modifies all optional fields and one that modifies none, to verify correct behavior at both boundaries.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41936
**Modules:** model-serving

---

### 949. Test Assertions Must Verify The Specific Code Path
**Rule:** When multiple code paths can trigger similar behavior, make test assertions specific enough (e.g., checking message content or parameters) to verify the expected path was exercised, not just that a side effect occurred.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42446, #47209
**Modules:** workspace-all, extensions
**Trigger:** You're writing a test for behavior that can be triggered by multiple code paths, such as error handling, logging, or notification logic where different conditions produce similar side effects.
**Rationale:** When tests only verify that a side effect occurred without checking specifics, they silently pass even if the wrong code path was exercised. This masks regressions where the intended logic breaks but another path coincidentally produces a similar outcome.

### 955. Test Rollback Order And Failure
**Rule:** When implementing rollback or cleanup stacks, write tests that verify execution order and behavior when individual rollback callbacks throw errors.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42117
**Modules:** model-serving
**Trigger:** You're implementing a rollback or cleanup stack (e.g., resource teardown after a failed deployment or model serving operation) and need to verify that callbacks execute in the correct order and that a failure in one callback doesn't prevent the remaining callbacks from running.
**Rationale:** Rollback stacks protect against resource leaks during partial failures in model serving deployments. If execution order is wrong or a single callback failure aborts the remaining cleanups, orphaned resources (GPUs, endpoints, model artifacts) accumulate silently in production and are costly to detect and remediate.

---

### 960. Track Partial Failures In Response
**Rule:** When an operation can partially succeed, return what was included and what failed to include with reasons, rather than failing the entire request or silently dropping items.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40750, #47263
**Modules:** projects
**Trigger:** You're writing an endpoint or service method that processes a collection of items where individual items can fail independently (e.g., bulk imports, multi-resource operations, batch updates). You need to decide how to handle cases where some items succeed and others fail.
**Rationale:** Silently dropping failed items leaves users confused about missing data, while failing the entire request forces users to retry everything even when most items succeeded. Reporting both successes and itemized failures gives callers the information they need to resolve issues without repeating work that already completed.

### 964. Understand Component Mounting Scope Before Adding Guards
**Rule:** Before adding authentication redirects or route guards to a React component, verify the component's mounting lifecycle — if it is always mounted while the app is active, the guard may unintentionally affect unrelated routes.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41116, #45259
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're adding an authentication redirect, route guard, or conditional navigation logic to a React component. You need to verify whether the component is scoped to a specific route or mounted globally across the entire application.
**Rationale:** Components mounted at the app shell level persist across all route transitions, so guards placed inside them will intercept navigation for every route — not just the one you intended to protect. This causes unexpected redirects on public pages and makes auth behavior extremely difficult to debug.

### 967. Update All Consumers Of Shared Configuration
**Rule:** When modifying or renaming a shared configuration file, audit and update every service that includes or depends on that file to avoid runtime failures.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41449, #43081
**Modules:** nucleus, compute-providers
**Trigger:** You're modifying, renaming, or restructuring a shared configuration file (e.g., a .conf, .yaml, or shared Typesafe Config include) that multiple services or modules reference. This also applies when changing configuration keys, removing sections, or altering default values in files that are included across service boundaries.
**Rationale:** Shared configuration creates invisible coupling between services — a rename or removal that looks local can trigger runtime failures in any service that includes the file. These failures typically surface only during deployment or integration testing, making them expensive to diagnose and fix under pressure.

### 992. Use Functional Error Types For Partial Matching
**Rule:** When a function may not handle all input cases, return a functional error type like Try or Either instead of throwing exceptions for unmatched cases.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41126
**Modules:** common-core
**Trigger:** You're writing a function that uses pattern matching or conditional logic but doesn't cover all possible input cases. You're tempted to throw an exception or use a catch-all that throws for unhandled variants.
**Rationale:** Throwing exceptions for expected unhandled cases hides failure modes from callers and bypasses the type system. Functional error types like Either make partiality visible at compile time, forcing callers to handle missing cases explicitly and preventing unexpected runtime crashes in production.

---

### 1007. Validate Input Edge Cases In Url And String Formats
**Rule:** When validating user-supplied strings like URLs, check for degenerate patterns such as consecutive dots, leading/trailing special characters, and other edge cases beyond basic regex matching.

**Category:** security
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #46554, #44769
**Modules:** gen-ai, server
**Trigger:** You're writing or reviewing validation logic for user-supplied strings such as URLs, hostnames, email addresses, or identifiers. You need to ensure malformed inputs with subtle edge cases (consecutive dots, trailing slashes, embedded whitespace) don't slip through basic regex checks.
**Rationale:** Basic regex patterns like `[\w.-]+` silently accept degenerate inputs such as `my..host.com` or `-leading.com`, which can cause downstream DNS resolution failures, security bypasses through host confusion, or hard-to-diagnose runtime errors. Explicit edge-case checks catch these at the validation boundary where error messages are most actionable.

---

### 1008. Use Optional Chaining For Nullable Function Calls
**Rule:** Use optional chaining syntax (e.g., `fn?.()`) instead of explicit null checks before invoking optional callback functions.

**Category:** code-organization
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #45132, #44629
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 1009. Use Optional Parameters With Sensible Defaults
**Rule:** When a function parameter has an obvious default value used by most callers, make it optional with that default rather than requiring every caller to pass it explicitly.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40619, #46145
**Modules:** frontend, modelmanager

---

### 1010. Use Polymorphism Over Optional Fields For Variants
**Rule:** When different entity variants have distinct sets of fields, use type polymorphism (subclasses or sealed traits) rather than making all variant-specific fields optional on a single type, to enforce invariants at the type level.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40794
**Modules:** projects
**Trigger:** You're defining a data model where different variants of an entity carry distinct sets of fields, and you're tempted to put all variant-specific fields as Optional on a single shared type.
**Rationale:** Optional fields for variants push invariant enforcement from compile time to runtime, making it possible to construct objects in illegal states. Polymorphic types let the compiler guarantee that each variant carries exactly the fields it needs, eliminating an entire class of bugs and making the code self-documenting.

---

### 1011. Use Px Units Not Em For Sizing
**Rule:** Express sizes in px rather than em to maintain consistency with the codebase's established convention; do not introduce em without a specific justification.

**Category:** code-organization
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42106
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 1012. Use React Useid For Unique Ids
**Rule:** Use React's useId() hook to generate stable, unique IDs for DOM elements instead of manual ID generation to avoid collisions and hydration mismatches.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41610
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 1013. Use Reduce Over Foreach For Accumulation
**Rule:** Use reduce instead of forEach when building or accumulating a result, as it makes the data transformation intent explicit and avoids side-effect-driven mutation.

**Category:** code-organization
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42418
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 1014. Use Refetch Over Local State Copy
**Rule:** Use the refetch method from data fetching hooks (e.g., useRemoteData) instead of copying remote data into local state to trigger re-renders after mutations.

**Category:** architecture
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42071
**Modules:** frontend

---

### 1015. Use Resizeobserver For Viewport Dependent Layout
**Rule:** When computing layout values based on viewport size, use a ResizeObserver instead of a one-time measurement so the layout updates when the viewport is resized.

**Category:** performance
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #42150
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** You're computing layout values (widths, heights, offsets) based on the current viewport or container size, such as dynamically sizing a panel, chart, or table to fit available space.
**Rationale:** A one-time measurement captures the viewport size only at mount, so the layout becomes stale if the user resizes the browser, toggles a sidebar, or switches between monitors. ResizeObserver reacts to any dimension change on the observed element, keeping the layout correct without polling or listening to the coarser window resize event.

---

**Rule:** Use Scala's idiomatic collection methods (e.g. isEmpty, nonEmpty) instead of manual equivalents for cleaner, more readable code.

**Category:** code-organization
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40765

### 1035. Use Vite Env Mode
**Rule:** In Vite apps, use `import.meta.env.MODE` to check the application mode instead of `process.env.NODE_ENV`, and use strict equality (===) for safety.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41584
**Modules:** frontend
**Trigger:** When checking the application environment (development vs. production) in a Vite-based frontend application
**Rationale:** Without using import.meta.env.MODE, the code relies on process.env.NODE_ENV which is not natively available in Vite's ESM environment and may be undefined or incorrectly polyfilled, causing environment checks to silently fail.

### 1040. Validate Entity State Before State Transitions
**Rule:** Before performing a state-changing operation (e.g., cleanup, cancellation), assert that the entity is in a valid state for that transition to avoid corrupting data.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #46129, #46533
**Modules:** dataset-rw, gen-ai
**Trigger:** You're writing code that performs a state-changing operation on an entity (e.g., canceling a job, cleaning up a dataset, transitioning a workspace lifecycle stage). The operation assumes the entity is in a particular state but doesn't explicitly verify it first.
**Rationale:** Performing state transitions without validating the source state can silently corrupt entity data — for example, re-cancelling a completed job may trigger cleanup that deletes valid output artifacts. Explicit guards catch programming errors and race conditions early, preserving data integrity and producing clear diagnostics instead of downstream mysteries.

---

### 1041. Validate File Before Operating
**Rule:** In build and CI scripts, check that required files (JARs, binaries, config files) exist before performing operations on them, and exit with a clear error message if missing.

**Category:** error-handling
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #47020, #44975
**Modules:** build, server

---

**Category:** api-design

### 1049. Verify All Consumers Of Shared Test Files
**Rule:** When modifying a shared test data file or fixture, verify that all other tests depending on that file still pass and are unaffected.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41418
**Modules:** e2e-tests
**Trigger:** You're modifying a shared test data file, fixture, or test utility that is imported or referenced by multiple test suites. This includes changes to JSON fixtures, shared helper functions, factory definitions, or common test configuration files.
**Rationale:** Shared test files create implicit coupling between test suites. Modifying them without verifying all consumers leads to cascading test failures that are discovered late, often by other team members in CI, wasting time and eroding trust in the test suite.

---

### 1050. Verify Auth Checks On New Service Endpoints
**Rule:** When exposing a new endpoint or service method, explicitly verify that appropriate authentication and authorization checks are in place, even when adapting code from existing patterns.

**Category:** security
**Mode:** hook
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41485
**Modules:** projects
**Trigger:** You're exposing a new endpoint or service method, or adapting an existing endpoint pattern to serve a new resource. This also applies when adding routes to an existing controller or creating a new gRPC/REST service method.
**Rationale:** New endpoints copied from existing patterns frequently ship without auth checks because developers assume the framework handles it automatically, or they copy a boilerplate route that used a less restrictive action builder. A single unprotected endpoint can expose sensitive project data or allow unauthorized mutations, making this a critical security review checkpoint.

---

### 1051. Verify Authorization Scope With Pm
**Rule:** When modifying role-based access controls, explicitly verify with the product manager which user tiers (e.g., lite users) should or should not have access to the affected endpoints.

**Category:** security
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41605
**Modules:** server

---

### 1052. Verify Backward Compatibility On Additive Dto Changes
**Rule:** When adding fields to a DTO or API response, verify that all downstream clients are tolerant of extra fields before merging.

**Category:** api-design
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41826
**Modules:** jobs
**Trigger:** You're adding a new field to a DTO, API response, or case class that is serialized and consumed by downstream clients. You assume additive changes are safe, but clients with strict deserialization may reject unknown fields.
**Rationale:** Additive DTO changes are only safe when all consumers ignore unknown fields. If even one downstream client uses strict deserialization, an added field can cause runtime failures in production — turning a seemingly harmless change into an outage.

---

### 1056. Verify Invariants When Changing Existing Logic

### 1057. Verify Logger Context In Nested Functions
**Rule:** When adding logging inside nested or helper functions, verify the logger is within scope and actually producing output, as loggers invoked inside nested functions can silently fail.

**Category:** logging
**Mode:** ambient
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #41521
**Modules:** system-tests
**Trigger:** You're adding logging statements inside nested functions, closures, or helper methods that are called from a parent function, and you need to ensure the logger is properly scoped and producing output.
**Rationale:** Loggers invoked inside nested functions or closures can silently fail to produce output when the logger instance is improperly scoped, uses an unexpected logger name, or isn't captured by the closure. This leads to missing diagnostic information during debugging and incident response, making failures much harder to trace.

### 1059. Verify Parallel Test Isolation
**Rule:** When tests modify shared configuration (feature flags, system settings), verify that the changes are scoped so they do not interfere with other tests running in parallel.

**Category:** testing
**Mode:** active
**Confidence:** 0.2
**Review count:** 2
**Source PRs:** #40633
**Modules:** system-tests
**Trigger:** You're writing or modifying a test that changes shared configuration such as feature flags, system settings, or global state. This is especially critical when tests run in parallel via a shared test environment.
**Rationale:** Shared mutable state is the primary source of flaky tests in parallel execution. Unscoped configuration changes create ordering-dependent failures that are difficult to reproduce locally and erode team confidence in the test suite.
